## Transcript

- [00:00] So far in our exploration of how the mind grows,
- [00:03] we've talked about a lot of different philosophies and models and ways of looking at things.
- [00:07] But here's a surprisingly useful tool for understanding developmental psychology:
- [00:11] The Breakfast Club. This video, by the way, will contain Breakfast Club spoilers.
- [00:15] That classic 80s movie about a band of teenagers
- [00:18] stuck in detention one fateful Saturday morning.
- [00:20] Do they do Saturday detentions anymore?
- [00:23] That was never a "thing" at my school.
- [00:25] That was crazy, the idea that kids would come in on a weekend for detention.
- [00:29] You got the hoodlum, the jock, the nerd, the princess, and the so-called basketcase,
- [00:33] And at first, they're all salty and standoffish with each other.
- [00:36] Because you know, let's face it, American high schools are sort of a breeding ground for that kind of thing.
- [00:39] But as the day progresses, they start to open up and share things
- [00:42] and have a little fun by way of a dance montage.
- [00:45] And at some point they each kind of crack, revealing something very important
- [00:48] about adolescence in the process, which is -
- [00:51] The struggle between the need to stand out, and the need to belong.
- [00:54] All these kids feel tremendous pressure to maintain their image in their particular group
- [00:59] in part because there's just some security in belonging to a group,
- [01:02] even if that group gets picked on by another group.
- [01:05] And so they wear the corresponding diamond earrings,
- [01:07] combat boots, lettermans jackets, and spectacles, and act how their roles dictate.
- [01:12] But the thing is, none of those kids are satisfied with their outward identities.
- [01:16] Instead, they're all stuck in the classic teenage struggle,
- [01:19] one that German psychoanalyst Erik Erikson -
- [01:21] yes, his name is actually Erik Erikson -
- [01:23] called the crisis between identity and role confusion.
- [01:27] In other words, their newly-forming identities were at odds
- [01:30] with other people's expectations of what roles they should play, hence the confusion.
- [01:34] The past couple weeks, we've been talking about childhood as
- [01:37] a crucial period of growth, learning, and change, and it definitely is,
- [01:41] but anyone who's ever seen a John Hughes movie knows
- [01:43] that there's way more to growing up than just that.
- [01:46] And I got some news for you -
- [01:48] from adolescence into adulthood and into old age,
- [01:51] the drama of your personal psychological development never ceases.
- [01:54] Never! So get used to it.
- [01:57] [INTRO]
- [02:06] Today, most psychologists view our psychological development as a lifelong process
- [02:10] from infant, to adolescent, to adult,
- [02:12] to card-carrying senior citizen, people keep right on changing.
- [02:16] Just as Piaget gave us a helpful framework for thinking about early development,
- [02:20] other scholars have given us ideas about how we develop
- [02:23] through the rest of our lives, particularly Erik Erikson.
- [02:26] Like many others, Erikson believed our personalities develop in a predetermined order,
- [02:30] which he outlined in an eight-stage model.
- [02:32] And each stage, from infancy to old age, is defined by
- [02:35] its own predominant issue or crisis.
- [02:38] If it freaks you out to think that you will always be dealing with a crisis
- [02:41] at every stage of your life, we can just call them "issues."
- [02:44] Since we already talked a lot about early childhood development,
- [02:47] I'm gonna skip ahead to the teenage meat of it.
- [02:49] But you can consult this table to learn about the rest.
- [02:52] Erikson defined the adolescent years, or "Stage 5,"
- [02:54] as starting in our teens, and extending for some as far as our early twenties.
- [02:58] And as The Breakfast Club so artfully depicted,
- [03:01] its main crisis is the one of identity vs. role confusion.
- [03:04] Teen years are marked by lots of physical changes
- [03:07] in the body and brain and sex hormones, along with growing independence,
- [03:11] but also a real need to belong to something.
- [03:14] This often angsty time is when teens reexamine their identities,
- [03:18] figuring out how to both blend in and how to stand out,
- [03:21] often by trying on different roles.
- [03:23] Maybe they're experimenting with punk rock,
- [03:25] or hockey, or theater, or ancient philosophy.
- [03:28] Maybe sophomore year they're preppy. Junior year they've got green hair.
- [03:31] Hopefully a person comes out of this stage with a reintegrated sense of self,
- [03:34] but this stage can be particularly confusing as I'm sure anybody watching this video can attest to.
- [03:39] But of course, that's not the last crisis - sorry, "issue."
- [03:43] Erikson believed that young adulthood, which in his view,
- [03:45] started in a person's 20s and ended as late as the early 40s,
- [03:49] was marked by another struggle, one between intimacy and isolation.
- [03:52] By this stage in life, most of us have begun exploring intimate relationships,
- [03:56] whether that's with a steady sweetheart, or just an active OkCupid profile.
- [03:59] A good relationship here can lead to feelings of safety and caring and commitment,
- [04:03] while a lack of intimacy can lead to loneliness and isolation and depression.
- [04:06] Recently, a number of psychologists have begun to refer to the first few years of this stage as
- [04:10] emerging adulthood, and some suggest that it warrants its own classification
- [04:14] distinct from adolescence or full adulthood.
- [04:17] And at least in modern western culture, many people in this stage
- [04:20] do feel like they're stuck in a sort of in-between time.
- [04:23] They know that they've pulled through all that high school stuff, but they're still pretty tied to their families.
- [04:26] In 2011, the U.S. Census found that 65% of people under 24 still live with their parents.
- [04:32] Just a reminder of how things like economic factors can weigh on development.
- [04:35] For Erikson, after young adulthood came the middle adulthood of our 40s to 60s.
- [04:40] This stage, Stage 7, highlights our tendency toward either generativity or stagnation.
- [04:45] By now, many people have established jobs or careers or perhaps families of their own.
- [04:49] We better understand the bigger picture of life and contribute to society
- [04:52] through productive, or generative, activities
- [04:54] like work, community involvement, raising kids, paying taxes, all that grown-up stuff.
- [04:59] The lack of those things, an overall boredom or absence of purpose,
- [05:02] can make Stage 7-ers feel stagnant and unproductive,
- [05:06] hence the often cliched, but really real and potentially painful, mid-life crisis.
- [05:10] And finally, at the end, comes Stage 8.
- [05:13] In our late adulthood, from 65 and up, we often struggle with integrity vs. despair.
- [05:18] Maybe you've hung out with a grandparent or some other senior and heard them contemplating
- [05:21] their lives and accomplishments and reminiscing about how cheap a milkshake used to be.
- [05:25] Well, if their overall vibe is positive,
- [05:28] they've probably developed a sense of integrity and completeness,
- [05:31] meaning they're pretty satisfied with a life well lived.
- [05:33] The flip side of that is looking back on life and feeling guilt and regret, and that kind of
- [05:37] retrospective disappointment can ruin old age with depression and feelings of hopelessness.
- [05:41] Again, Erikson's model isn't really a perfect contemporary one,
- [05:45] but it gave us an early idea of conflict and growth over our whole lives.
- [05:49] His ideas have been developed further and even challenged by other scholars,
- [05:53] but like Piaget, he remains a crucial figure to know in Western psychology.
- [05:57] So Erikson tackled our progressive psychosocial development,
- [06:00] but what exactly happens to our bodies and brains after we hit adulthood
- [06:04] and keep racking up the birthday cake candles?
- [06:06] It's hard to generalize these stages of adulthood because
- [06:08] we don't really hit yearly milestones like we did when we were kids,
- [06:11] and adult lifestyles can vary a whole lot. I mean, in a lot of ways,
- [06:14] 70-year-old Mick Jagger's still living a younger lifestyle than a lot of 20-somethings I know.
- [06:18] But despite all our differences, many of our life courses do have some similarities --
- [06:22] physically, cognitively, and socially.
- [06:24] First, there are, yes, physical changes:
- [06:26] the slow decline of reaction time, muscle tone, and strength,
- [06:29] cardiac output, sex hormone production,
- [06:32] and sharpness of senses like hearing and sight.
- [06:34] For most of us, bifocals are inevitable, and perhaps hearing aids as well.
- [06:38] None of this is to say that a jacked 50-year-old couldn't beat a lazy 20-year-old on a 100-meter dash,
- [06:43] because, of course, how well you take care of your body counts for a lot.
- [06:46] But still, you can't stop, let alone reverse the process of aging.
- [06:50] The good news is our intelligence remains pretty stable throughout adulthood.
- [06:54] Although some people might feel that their wits get a bit fuzzy with age,
- [06:57] research suggests that while one kind of intelligence decreases after adolescence,
- [07:01] another kind keeps increasing throughout your lifetime.
- [07:04] Psychologists Raymond Cattell and John Horn were the first to develop the concepts of
- [07:07] fluid and crystallized intelligence, suggesting that
- [07:10] intelligence itself is made up of different abilities that work together.
- [07:14] Fluid intelligence deals with your ability to solve problems
- [07:17] independent of your personal experience and education.
- [07:19] It's typically associated with thinking both quickly and abstractly,
- [07:23] like teasing out the logic of a puzzle, rather than
- [07:25] remembering how to find the cosine of an angle.
- [07:28] So relatively inexperienced teens often show high fluid intelligence.
- [07:32] The bummer is it peaks in adolescence,
- [07:35] then typically starts its slow decline in the 30s...
- [07:38] So I'm experiencing that now...
- [07:40] Crystallized intelligence, on the other hand, is just what it sounds like:
- [07:43] knowledge that's based on facts, solidified by past experiences and prior learning.
- [07:47] This type of experiential intelligence gets stronger with age,
- [07:50] as we continue to take in new knowledge and understanding,
- [07:53] and it's part of why grandmas are so good at crossword puzzles!
- [07:56] Both fluid and crystallized intelligence are equally important on any given day,
- [08:00] and ideally, they work together to get the job of thinking done.
- [08:04] So in the end, some of our thinking gets rusty with age, but some of it keeps getting better.
- [08:09] Of course, there are exceptions.
- [08:11] While most people who live into their 90s are still pretty sharp,
- [08:14] some will experience a substantial loss of brain cells and suffer serious consequences.
- [08:19] Brain tumors, small strokes, or continued alcohol dependence
- [08:22] can all progressively damage the brain, increasing the risk of dementia.
- [08:25] Dementia isn't a specific disease, but rather a set of symptoms related to
- [08:29] impaired thinking, memory loss, confusion, and potential changes in personality
- [08:32] that become severe enough to interfere with regular functioning.
- [08:36] Alzheimer's disease is a form of progressive, irreversible dementia.
- [08:39] First, memory declines, then reasoning, and then eventually basic physiological functions
- [08:44] as vital brain neurons continue to deteriorate.
- [08:46] It strikes about 3% of the world's population before age 75,
- [08:50] although from there, the rate roughly doubles every five years.
- [08:53] But again, not all dementia is related to Alzheimer's disease, nor is it as extreme.
- [08:58] And while the risk of dementia certainly increases in older adults,
- [09:01] it's important to remember that it is not part of normal, healthy aging.
- [09:05] Some memory changes are normal, but most memories should remain intact.
- [09:08] In the end, we still have a lot to learn about the aging process.
- [09:11] As our lifespans continue to get longer, we might need to
- [09:14] tweak what we think we know about its effects on human psychology.
- [09:17] In some ways, you might say that this is psychology's next frontier.
- [09:20] By the time we figure out what that looks like, the cast of The Breakfast Club might just be
- [09:23] ready for a reunion... and they'd better make a movie about it.
- [09:26] Today, your developing brain learned about
- [09:28] Erikson's eight stages of progressive psychosocial development and their accompanying issues.
